Preface
This thesis is submitted as partial fulﬁlment of the requirements for the degree of Master of Science
in Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The thesis is written by Wenjun Xuan
and Si↵Luna Brask and is credited to 32.5 ECTS points to each author. The theme was suggested by
supervisor Linh Cao Hoang, who has kindly guided the authors through the whole project. Further-
more, Kasper Paaske Larsen from Rambøll has been helpful to the project. We want to thank both
for their valuable knowledge sharing and guidance.

Si↵Luna Brask (s133817)

ii
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
The plastic theory has in many cases been found useful when determining the load carrying capacity
of ductile structures and materials. In this study, the theory was applied for orthogonal reinforced
concrete panels. Though concrete is not a perfectly plastic material, and the concrete strength should
thereby be reduced by an e↵ectiveness factor. A recently proposed equation for the e↵ectiveness factor
was thereby tested. The equation is based on two reduction factors; reduction due to the brittleness of
concrete and reduction due to the interaction loss between steel and concrete. Di↵erent combinations of
these reduction factors were tested, to ﬁnd suitable brittleness- and interaction factors. The evaluation
of the proposed equation was based on an empirical approach, where data from various experiments
were used to verify the conducted theoretical model. In total 72 panels was investigated in the thesis.
In the study, it was found that the proposed equation seemed to have a rather good ﬁt since maximum
shear stresses found for eight di↵erent combinations all had a mean close to 1 when dividing the
experimental shear stress with the predicted shear stress. Besides that, all standard deviations were
found to be below 0.19. The best ﬁt obtained from the load case pure shear was
⌫1 =
3
r30
fc
·
1
1.2 + 80"1
The best ﬁt obtained from combined shear and biaxial compression/tension was
⌫6 =
3
r40
fc
·
1
1 + 90"1
and the best ﬁt found for all load cases combined was
⌫5 =
3
r30
fc
·
1
1 + 110"1
Additionally, a method of calculation was conducted to estimate the maximum shear strength in the
panel, and it was showed, that the results were similar to the theoretical model.
Furthermore, a parametric study of the ductility index showed, that the highest ductility index was
obtained by a combination between high concrete strengths, class C steel, low steel strength and low
reinforcement ratios. It was also seen, that orthotropic reinforced panels had a higher ductility index
than isotropic reinforced panels. Though, based on the obtained maximum shear strain results from
experiment and model, it was found that the ﬁt was not that well. Therefore, an evaluation of the
ductility of a structure or material should not be based on the used theory used to obtain the model
as it is now.

Chapter 1
Introduction
In this thesis, rigid-plastic models will be used to determine the load carrying capacity of reinforced
concrete structures. When applying rigid-plastic theory, it is necessary to reduce the compressive
strength of concrete by an e↵ectiveness factor.
This factor accounts for the fact that concrete is
not perfectly plastic material. There are many e↵ects that inﬂuence the reduction of strength. This
includes the e↵ect of crack sliding when redistribution of concrete stresses take place and the e↵ect of
internal cracking due to tensile strains transverse to the compression stress ﬁeld. From experiments, it
has been shown that both of these e↵ects can be expressed in terms of the average transverse strains
in the reinforced members. To account for the e↵ect of transverse strains on the e↵ective strength,
it is necessary to adopt method of calculations that can model the load-deformation relationship of
the reinforced members. In this project, the stress ﬁelds assumed in rigid plastic models will be used
in combination with compatibility requirements to establish the complete load-deformation curve for
panels loaded in shear and combined shear-compression/tension. The theory is presented in the ﬁrst
part of the thesis.
After that, the experimental data used in the thesis is presented. No tests are performed in this
project, but experimental data will be collected from various experiments. In total 72 orthogonal
reinforced panels was collected. The panels were subjected to pure shear and combined shear and
biaxial compression/tension. The experimental data consists of 60 panels subjected to pure shear, six
panels subjected to combined shear and biaxial compression and four panels subjected to combined
shear and biaxial tension.
The models, which will account for the elastic phase; the partial plastic phase; and the ultimate plastic
phase will then be presented and used as a basis for an evaluation of a recently proposed equation
for the e↵ectiveness factor. The evaluation will be done by comparing tests with calculations. The
analyses to be carried out will enable an assessment of the load-carrying capacity. It is estimated
whether a combination of the proposed e↵ectiveness factor equation can lead to one formula that ﬁts
both the case of pure shear as well as the case of combined shear and compression/tension. It will
also be tested if a good ﬁt can be found for the two loading cases each.
Additionally, a theoretical method of calculation based on iteration will be tested, to see how well the
method complies with the modelled load-deformation curve when estimating the shear stress capacity.
The method of calculation is far more suitable for practical use, and it is therefore tested as an
alternative method.
Lastly, the ductility will be investigated, both as a parametric study and also to see how well the
model predicts the shear strain at failure. If the prediction is found to be somewhat precise, it could
mean that certain structures could be optimised even more.

Chapter 2
Theory
2.1
Theory of plasticity
In this section, the failure criteria of concrete will be presented. The failure criteria for concrete are not
possible to establish from known natural laws, and the failure criteria, therefore, have to be developed
from hypotheses and later veriﬁed by experiments [8]. Next, the yield condition for steel and Von
Mises’s ﬂow rule are described, and lastly, the yield condition for orthogonal reinforced concrete is
established.
2.1.1
Failure criteria of concrete
By combining Coulomb’s frictional hypothesis with a bound for the maximum tensile stress, the
resulting failure criteria become known as Coulomb Material and Modiﬁed Coulomb Material. These
describe the failure modes of sliding failure and separation failure, which are failure modes that contain
reasonable compliance with the failure modes of concrete.
Sliding failure will happen when the shear stress is equal to the sliding resistance. This is given as [7]
|⌧| = c −µσ
(2.1)
Where c denotes the cohesion, and µ denotes the angle of friction. Both are positive constants and
the stress, σ, is positive for tensile stresses.
Separation failure will occur when the tensile stress in a section exceeds the separation resistance,
given as [7]
σ = fA
(2.2)
Materials complying with Equation (2.1) is known as Coulomb Materials, where else materials com-
plying with both Equation (2.1) and (2.2) are known as Modiﬁed Coulomb Materials. The failure
criteria of the conditions above, thus for a Modiﬁed Coulomb Material, are seen in Figure 2.1.

When using plastic models on concrete, it is on the safe side to set the tensile strength equal to zero,
since the tensile failure of concrete is far from ductile. The inﬂuence of the ductility of concrete is
further discussed in Chapter 6.
2.1.2
Von Mises’s ﬂow rule
Von Mises’s ﬂow rule is applicable to rigid-plastic materials. Rigid-plastic materials are deﬁned as
materials with no deformations until the material reaches its yield point. Hereafter, the material will
increasingly deform under constant stress [7]. The ideal stress-strain curve of a rigid-plastic material
is seen in Figure 2.4.
fY;t
fY;c
σ
"
Figure 2.4: Rigid-plastic behaviour
Such material does not exist. Though, some materials, including steel, approximate this behaviour,
since the plastic strains are signiﬁcantly larger than the elastic strains.
This is not quite the case for concrete, but it has been shown that the rigid-plastic assumptions
in many cases predict well with load-carrying capacities for reinforced structures when applying the
e↵ectiveness factor [7]. Though, it should be noted, that even though the capacities has shown to be
reasonably precise, the deformations have not. This is further discussed in Chapter 6.
5
==PAGE:21==
Von Mises’s ﬂow rule assumes that arbitrarily large strains will occur with no change in stresses at
the point of yielding [7]. The yield condition can thus be speciﬁed as
f(σx, σy, .., ⌧zy) = 0
(2.3)
Where σx, σy, .., ⌧zy denotes the generalised stresses in a structure. Furthermore, it is assumed that
f<0 consists of stresses that can be sustained in the body, while f>0 cannot occur [7].
The total work, D, needed to deform a rigid-plastic body is given as [7]
D =
Z
V
(σx"x + σy"y...)dV =
Z
V
WdV
(2.4)
Where V is the volume, area or length of the structure, " is the generalised strains, and W is the work
per unit volume, area or length. From the equation above it is given that
W = σx"x + σy"y... = ¯σ¯"
(2.5)
The ﬂow rule is based on the von Mises’s hypothesis on maximum work, which states that the stress
ﬁeld should be chosen, so that the work, W, will become as large as possible [7].
By combining
Equation (2.3) with Equation (2.5) it can be found that when the work, W, is stationary, the strains,
¯", must be normal to the yield surface [7]. This is written as
¯" = λδf
δ¯σ
(2.6)
Here it is assumed, that the yield surface is di↵erentiable without plane surfaces or apexes and that
the yield surface is convex and closed [7].
Equation (2.6) is known as the von Mises’s ﬂow rule or the normality condition. Since W is assumed
to be non-negative, ¯" will become an outward-directed normal to the yield surface as seen on Figure
2.5 [7].

2.1.3
Yield condition for reinforced panels
Reinforced concrete is a composite material consisting of two materials; steel and concrete. A yield
condition for the composite material should thereby be developed by combining the yield condition
and material behaviour examined in the previous chapters for the two basic materials. In reality,
this is not as simple, since theory and reality do not correspond that well in detail. Luckily, a more
accurate description of the detailed behaviour is not necessary to determine the load-carrying capacity
of a reinforced structure [7].
Therefore, the failure criteria for concrete described in Section 2.1.1 will be used as a yield condition
for concrete. By combining this with the rigid-plastic material behaviour of steel a yield condition
can be described for the composite material obeying the normality condition and have convex yield
surface.
The concrete will be assumed to have zero tensile strength, due to the brittle behaviour. Furthermore,
the reinforcement is assumed to only consist of straight bars with stress-strain relation as seen in
Figure 2.4. The bars are assumed to carry only average longitudinal tensile and compressive stresses.
This will secure values only on the safe side when deducing the load-carrying capacity [7].
Given these conditions and assumptions, a yield condition for an orthogonal reinforced panel can be
described as
σx ftx
σy fty
σx ≥−fcx
σy ≥−fcy
−(ftx −σx)(ftx −σy) + ⌧2
xy 0
−(fc + σx)(fc + σy) + ⌧2
xy 0
(2.7)
where σx and σy are the stresses in the x- and y-direction, ftx and fty are the tensile strengths in the x-
and y-direction, fc is the concrete strength and ⌧xy is the shear stress. While for higher reinforcement
degree an upper limit is introduced as
|⌧xy| 1
2fc
The deduction of the yield condition is described in more detail in [7]. The tensile strength is equivalent
to

(2.9)
where Φx and Φy are the reinforcement degrees. This parameter is more thoroughly described in
Section 4.1.

2.2
Fundamental principles of mechanics of materials
In this chapter, the fundamental principles of mechanics of materials are presented.
Firstly, the
constitute equations are explained for steel and concrete. Afterwards, the compatibility requirements
for the panel are found using Mohr’s circle of strains. Then the equilibrium equations described by
the total stresses are presented. Lastly, the inclination of the concrete stress ﬁeld is expressed in terms
of tan(✓).
2.2.1
Constitutive equations
Constitutive equations describe the relation between the average stresses and the average strains
for the concrete and the reinforcement.
The average stress-strain relationship in the panel could
vary from normal material test values for concrete and steel due to the bond between concrete and
reinforcement and the amount of reinforcement distributed in the panels. For simplicity, it is assumed
that the average stress-strain relationship for the concrete and the reinforcement are independent of
each other. Furthermore, the shear stresses orthogonal to the reinforcement are assumed to be zero.
The average normal stresses in the reinforcement are assumed to depend solely on the normal strain
[13]. According to Vecchio and Collins [13] the average reinforcement stress-strain relationship follows
the simple bi-linear function deﬁned by the Young’s Modulus, Es shown in Figure 2.6.

direction, ⌧sx and ⌧sy are the shear stress in the two directions and Es is modulus of elasticity of
reinforcement. In the model, it is assumed that the concrete has inﬁnite strength, which means that

of concrete will therefore always follow the linear-elastic material behaviour

an identical deformation change in the reinforcement. Likewise, a change in the concretes strain must
result in an equal strain change in the reinforcement.

2.3
E↵ectiveness factor
The e↵ectiveness factor is introduced, when calculating on concrete structures using plastic theories.
When applying rigid-plastic theory on reinforced concrete, it is seen from Section 2.1.1 and 2.1.2 that
while steel applies well with the theory, concrete does not. This is due to the fact, that steel has a
rather ductile behaviour, while the strength of concrete decreases once the ultimate strength has been
reached. The low ductility of concrete is why concrete is commonly referred to as a brittle material.
In Figure 2.10 the behaviour of the two materials along with the adopted rigid-plastic behaviour is
illustrated.
σ
"
fy
≈10%
σ
"
νfc
≈0:5%
(a) (Mild)Steel
(b) Concrete
Figure 2.10: Stress-strain curves. The dotted line illustrates the adopted rigid-
plastic behaviour.
The low ductility of concrete mainly depends on the following factors [7]; softening, stressed reinforce-
ment going through internal cracks and sliding in initial cracks.
The e↵ectiveness factor, therefore, needs to be developed in a way, where these factors are taken
into account. In the recently proposed equation, the factors will be incorporated by the following
two reduction factors; Reduction due to the brittleness of concrete (⌘fc) and reduction due to the
interaction loss between steel and concrete (⌘").
2.3.1
Brittleness factor, ⌘fc
The brittleness of concrete highly depends on the strength. The higher the strength becomes, the
more brittle the material behaves, resulting in a low ductility. This is clearly seen, when plotting the
simpliﬁed empirical expression on the response of a standard concrete cylinder by Fern´andez et al. [5]
using di↵erent strengths. The expression is illustrated in Figure 2.11 and the empirical expression is
seen in Equation (2.38)-(2.40).
σc =
1

(2.38)

creased concrete strength
The descending branch, seen in Figure 2.11 after ultimate strength has occurred, is known as softening.
In the ﬁgure, it is seen that the softening strongly depends on the concrete strength. Here it is seen
that a strength of 20 MPa has a somewhat ductile behaviour, while a concrete with a strength of 80
MPa is very brittle. In order to compensate for the increasing brittleness with increasing strength,
the following expression was proposed
⌘fc = min

fc0 =30 MPa and fc0 =40 MPa. It can be questioned whether these strengths should be considered
ductile or not, and which one is more correct, given the stress-strain curves observed in 2.11. Though,
the descending branch depends on several parameters such as length along which the average strain
is measured, type of specimen and the conﬁnement [7]. Therefore, the descending branch might vary
from Figure 2.11, and an estimation of fc0 will be based on empirical data.
In Figure 2.12 it is seen how the two brittleness factors will di↵er from each other. The ﬁgure shows
that up until 30 MPa the reduction will be zero for both, hereafter will the two brittleness factors
start to vary from each other.

2.3.2
Interaction factor between steel and concrete, ⌘"
Reinforcement is often necessary to incorporate in concrete to obtain a satisfying strength and ductility.
However, the interaction between the two also means that the concrete strength reduces.
One reason is that the concrete matrix is discontinued due to the presence of rebars, which deviates
the compressive stress path [9].
Besides that, the concrete strength reduces when cracks appear. Cracks will always be present in
concrete, e.g. from shrinkage or loading. But they might be increased, when stressed reinforcement
are crossing macrocracks, since this will lead to a transfer of stresses from the reinforcement to the
concrete [7]. The stresses will then increase the number of microcracks. Since macrocracks and some
microcracks are load-induced, the cracking will lead to anisotropy, which means that the strength will
depend on the direction.
Furthermore, sliding in the initial cracks might lead to failure at a very low stress compared to the
original strength, for orthotropic panels in particular. This is due to the fact that the compression
direction will change during loading, i.e. the initial compression direction will be di↵erent from the
ﬁnal one [7]. The phenomenon is illustrated in Figure 2.13.
Final crack
direction
Initial crack
direction
45°
Figure 2.13: Orthotropic panel in pure shear [7]
The concrete strength should, therefore, be reduced to take care of the interaction losses. In the re-
cently proposed equation the reduction factor is suggested, see Equation (2.42), to take the parameters
mentioned above into account.
17
==PAGE:33==
⌘" =
1
k1 + k2 · "1
(2.42)
Where "1 is the transverse strain and the constants k1 and k2 are parameters which will be examined
and chosen based on the empirical data. In Figure 2.14 the di↵erent ⌘"-values tested in this study are
presented. Most k1-values are set to 1 to avoid reduction of panels in compression.
Figure 2.14: The interaction reduction factor between reinforcement and concrete
tested in this study
It is seen from Figure 2.14, that k1=1.2 will result in a higher interaction reduction factor between
the reinforcement and concrete, when no transverse strains are introduced in the panel. Furthermore,
it is observed that the ﬁrst part of the curve is steeper for all combinations. Likewise, it is seen that
a relatively higher k2-value will result in a steeper curve.
2.3.3
Proposed equations for the e↵ectiveness factor
The recently proposed equation for the e↵ectiveness factor will, therefore, be given as a combination of
the presented reduction factors accounting for the brittleness of concrete, ⌘fc, and for the interaction
losses between steel and concrete, ⌘".
⌫= ⌘fc⌘"
(2.43)
Recently, three di↵erent equations for the e↵ectiveness factor was proposed
⌫=

These three equations were tested as an e↵ectiveness factor in this thesis. Besides these, three addi-
tional combinations were suggested. The purpose was to evaluate the recently proposed equations for
the e↵ectiveness factor.
Furthermore, an parametric analysis was conducted to evaluate the k2-value and fc0. In the analysis,
three k2-values 50, 100 and 150 were chosen and three fc0-values 20 MPa, 30 MPa and 40 MPa.
k1-value was not evaluated, since the reduction of the panels compression strength, with no initial
principal tensile strain, was undesirable. The evaluation was based on the mean value and standard
deviation of the predicted shear strengths and a comparison of the observed failure from experiment
with the predicted failure found from the model. The analysis can be seen in Appendix B.
Therefore, the eight suggested e↵ectiveness factors that will be tested in this study are presented in
Table 2.1.

As seen in Table 2.1 the e↵ectiveness factors are comparable in the following way;
⌫1 and ⌫2 have equivalent fc0- and k2-values, but the k1-values di↵ers from each other. ⌫3, ⌫4 and ⌫5
have equivalent fc0-values and k1-values, but an increasing k2-value from 90 to 110. ⌫6, ⌫7 and ⌫8 also
have equivalent fc0-values and k1-values, but an increasing k2-value from 90 to 110. The di↵erence
between ⌫3-⌫5 and ⌫6-⌫8 is the fc0-value, where it is 30 MPa and 40 MPa, respectively. The equations
are thus also comparable between the six factors, for equivalent k2-values but di↵erent fc0-values. The
e↵ect of each parameter will, based on these comparisons, be investigated in the following chapters.

==PAGE:36==
Chapter 3
Experimental data
In this study, experimental data is collected from previously performed experiments. Firstly, a de-
scription of the various experiments are presented and observed failure modes are listed. Hereafter, a
summary of the panel material parameters collected from the di↵erent experiments are summarised
in tables. The data will be used as input parameters in the investigated calculation methods.
This thesis will only focus on three panel loading types; pure shear, combined shear and biaxial
compression and combined shear and biaxial tension. If any of the tests deviates from these load
types, they will be neglected in the study.
3.1
Description of experiments
In the following, a brief brush up is made of each experiment used in this study.
3.1.1
Collins & Vecchio
F. Vecchio and M. P. Collins published in 1982 a report [11] with experimental results from 30 panels
tested in pure shear and combined shear and biaxial stress. The panels were 890 x 890 x 70 mm and
typically reinforced with two layers of welded wire mesh. The reinforcement was placed parallel to
the sides of the panel, thereby forming an orthogonal grid, with 50 mm centres. Besides that, a 6 mm
cover between the face of the concrete and the longitudinal reinforcement was made. The mesh was
in many cases heat-treated to increase ductility of the reinforcement. The concrete strengths varied
between 11.6 MPa to 29.8 MPa.
Tests were made on the used concrete and steel to measure the strength of the materials. Before
the reinforced panels were tested in the test setup, Zurich gauges had been placed to measure strains
along the panel, and two LVDTs (Linear Variable Di↵erential Transformers) were placed to monitor
the edge displacement of the panels. Furthermore, a hydraulic pressure was used to control the stresses
applied. Five shear keys were placed on each side of the panel, where links in two directions were
connected to be able to apply the stresses, see Figure 3.1a and 3.1b.
21
==PAGE:37==
a) Five shear keys were placed on each side of the panel
b) Links were connected to each
shear key to be able to apply stresses
to the panel
Figure 3.1: Testing rig of the experiment of Collins and Vecchio [11]
For instance, pure shear was applied when the links were subjecting an equal pulling and pushing
force, as illustrated in Figure 3.1b.
In this study, only 17 out of 30 panels were used, due to some of them having failures, e.g. from local
premature failure. Out of the 17 panels, 13 were subjected to pure shear, three were subjected to
combined shear and biaxial compression (PV23, PV24 and PV25), and one was subjected to combined
shear and biaxial tension (PV28). Panels tested in shear and biaxial compression/tension were tested
with a ﬁxed ratio of normal to shear stresses (k), but the ratio varied between each tested panel. The
ratios can be observed in Table 3.6. In Table 3.1 the observed failures of the used panels can be seen.
Table 3.1: Observed failure modes of panels in the experiments of Collins and
Vecchio

3.1.2
Zhang & Hsu
In January 1998, L.-X. Zhang and T. T. C. Hsu published the article ”Behavior and Analysis of 100
MPa Concrete Membrane Elements”, see [16]. Here experimental data from 14 full-size reinforced
panels tested in a universal panel tester were presented.
All panels were cast with high-strength
concrete with a strength of approximately 100 MPa. The size of the panels was given as 1397 x 1397
x 178 mm except from VA4, which due to large rebars had a thickness of 152 mm.
The panels were divided into three series: VE-, VA- and VB-series. The VE-series, subjected to biaxial
straining, was placed with a 90◦angle between the longitudinal steel bars and the applied compressive
loads, as seen in Figure 3.2.
Figure 3.2: Placement of reinforcement in panels and applied stress direction of
VE-series
Where else the VA- and VB-series, subjected to pure shear, were tested with a 45◦angle between the
steel bars and the (2, 1)-coordinate system of the applied stresses, as seen in Figure 3.3.
Figure 3.3: Placement of reinforcement in panels and applied stress direction of
VA- and VB-series
23
==PAGE:39==
Due to the angle between the reinforcement and applied loads in the VE-series, this study only focuses
on the VA- and VB-series. The two series di↵er from each other from the fact that the VA-series are
isotropically reinforced, while the VB-series are orthotopically reinforced. Both series are reinforced
in two layers as seen in Figure 3.3.
The given parameters from the experiment can be found in Table 3.7. In Table 3.2 the observed failure
modes are stated.
Table 3.2: Observed failure modes of panels in the experiments of Zhang and Hsu

Observed failure mode

VB4
Yielding of steel in both directions + concrete crushing
3.1.3
Collins, Vecchio & Aspiotis
In 1994, F. J. Vecchio, M. P. Collins and J. Aspiotis published an article [14] with experimental
data from testing of 12 reinforced panels. All test panels were 890 x 890 x 70 mm with orthogonal
reinforcement in either one or two layers and a compressive strength of approximately 55 MPa. Two
series were tested: PHS- and PA-series. The PA-series had half the amount of reinforcement in the
longitudinal direction as the PHS-series had.
The panels were subjected to monotonic, proportional loads with various combinations of in-plane
shear and biaxial stress. The loading was momentarily halted several times to take measurements.
Displacement was during test measured with LVDTs on both faces of the panel, and strain gauges
measured strains in longitudinal, transverse and diagonal directions, on both sides, using 200-mm-
base-length 16-point-grids. Loads were determined from hydraulic pressure.
Six of the panels (PHS1, PHS2, PHS3, PHS8, PA1 and PA2) were tested in pure shear, while three
panels (PHS4, PHS5 and PHS10) were tested in shear and biaxial tension and three (PHS6, PHS7
and PHS9) in shear and biaxial compression. All tests failed from concrete crushing after yielding in
the transverse direction, as seen in Table 3.3.
24
==PAGE:40==

and Aspiotis

Observed failure mode

PHS6

PA2
Yielding of steel in one direction + concrete crushing
Panels tested in shear and biaxial stresses were tested with a ﬁxed ratio of normal to shear stresses of
±0.25.
One test, PHS1, was left out from this study since reinforcement was only placed in one direction. All
test parameters can be found in Table 3.6 and Table 3.7.
3.1.4
Vecchio & Chan
In 1990, F. J. Vecchio and C. C. L. Chan published an article [12] with experimental data for ten
reinforced panels.
The experiment consisted of three series, where two of these were panels with
perforations. These will not be included in this study. The last series of tests consisted of a solid
panel with the dimensions 890 x 890 x 70 mm and a homogeneous grid of reinforcement. All panels
were made with a normal-density concrete with a nominal strength of 25 MPa. They all had two
layers of reinforcement orthogonal to each other and parallel to the sides of the panel. The primary
di↵erence between the tests was the loading condition imposed, ranging from pure shear to combined
shear and biaxial compression or tension.
The test was made similar to that of Vecchio, Collins and Aspiotis with LVDTs measuring displace-
ment, Zurich gauges measuring the longitudinal, transverse and two diagonal directions using a 200
mm length grid system encompassing the central 600-mm square area of the panel. Pressure trans-
ducers, connected within the hydraulic system of the load facility, were used to determine the loads
applied to the panel. Besides that, an essentially uniform applied stress along the edges was per-
formed in a monotonically increasing edge load. Though, again, breaks of up to 30 min were held due
to readings of measurements.
Panels tested in combined shear and biaxial compression/tension was held with a constant ratio of
-0.39 for combined shear and biaxial compression and +0.32 for combined shear and biaxial tension.
Panel PC1 failed prematurely, and the test was repeated with panel PC1A. Thus, only PC1A was
used in this study and PC1 was thereby disregarded.
In Table 3.4 the failure modes can be seen.
25
==PAGE:41==
Table 3.4: Observed failure modes of panels in the experiments of Vecchio and Chan
Panel
Observed failure mode
PC1A
Yielding of steel in one direction + concrete crushing
PC4
Yielding of steel in one direction + concrete crushing
PC7
Yielding of steel in one direction + concrete crushing
3.1.5
Pang & Hsu
In 1995, X.-B. Pang and T. T. C. Hsu published an article [10] with experimental data from 13 full-
sized reinforced panels. The panel sizes were all 1400 x 1400 x 178 mm with reinforcement orthogonal
to each other but rotated 45◦with respect to the applied loading directions ((2, 1)-coordinate). The
concrete strength was approximately 42 MPa, and the nominal yield stress of reinforcing bars was 420
MPa.
The experiment was divided into three series: A-, B- and C-series.
Series A consisted of panels
in pure shear with isotropic reinforcement, series B included panels in pure shear with orthotropic
reinforcement and series C was identical to series A, but with a di↵erent load path. Series C was
neglected in this study, due to the change in loading.
The experiment was performed in the same universal panel testing machine from the University of
Houston as the one used in the experiment by Zhang and Hsu.
In Table 3.5 the failure mode of the panels are noted.
Table 3.5: Observed failure modes of panels in the experiments of Pang and Hsu

3.1.6
Naganuma & Yamaguchi
In 1991, K. Naganuma and T. Yamaguchi published an article [15] with experimental data from 32
panels tested in various ways. In this study, the focus will be on the 22 panels tested in pure shear.
The article only exists in a Japanese version, and thus how the experiments were performed, and if
any special failure mode were observed are unknown. The experimental data from the article can be
found in Table 3.7. 16 of the panels were isotropic reinforced, while the remaining six were orthotropic
reinforced. The concrete strengths varied between 19 MPa to 80 MPa.
Failure modes are all unknown.
26
==PAGE:42==
3.2
Summary of collected data
In Table 3.6 a summary of all used data from panels in combined shear and biaxial compression/tension
can be seen. Table 3.7 shows all used data from panels in pure shear.
If no E-modulus for steel was given in the articles, a value of 200000 MPa was assumed. The strength
of the steel fy was given in both directions (x- and y-direction) as well as the reinforcement ratio
in each direction (⇢x and ⇢y). Furthermore, the concrete strength fc and the shear stress and shear
strain failure were given for the panels. No ultimate shear strain for the steel was given, except the
experiment by Vecchio and Collins. Here the heat-treated reinforcement had an ultimate shear strain
of app. 0.125, while none heat-treated it was given as app. 0.014.

Chapter 4
Theoretic model
This chapter implements the theories from Chapter 2.
Two models are established for the shear
stress-shear strain relation by combining the stress ﬁeld assumed in the rigid-plastic models with the
fundamental principles of the mechanics of the materials. The models are divided into a model for
panels subjected to pure shear and one for panels subjected to combined shear and biaxial compres-
sion/tension. The models will account for the three phases in the response curve; the elastic, partial
plastic and ultimate plastic.
Firstly, the used formulas to establish the models are presented, while the actual scripts used to make
the plots in Matlab are found in Appendix D. Before presenting the obtained results, a description is
made of how to analyse the plots. Then the predicted shear stress capacity found in the models is
presented and compared to the actual test results.
4.1
Model implementation
This chapter will examine the theory used to implement the response curve of the reinforced panels
as well as the panel shear strength.
The study will be restricted solely to the study of concrete panels reinforced orthogonally, i.e. rein-
forced in two directions perpendicular to each other, in the (x, y)-direction. Di↵erent reinforcement
degrees will be examined. The reinforcement degree is given as
Φx = ⇢x · fyx
fc
(4.1)
Φy = ⇢y · fyy
fc
(4.2)
where Φx is the reinforcement degree in the x-direction, Φy is the reinforcement degree in the y-
direction, ⇢x is the reinforcement ratio for reinforcing steel in the x-direction, ⇢y is the reinforcement
ratio for reinforcing steel in the y-direction, fyx is the steel strength for the reinforcement in the
x-direction, fyy is the steel strength for the reinforcement in the y-direction and fc is the strength of
concrete. The reinforcement ratios are calculated as [6]
⇢x = Asx
t
(4.3)
31
==PAGE:47==
⇢y = Asy
t
(4.4)
where t is the thickness of the panel and Asx and Asy are given as the section areas of the reinforcement
in the x- and y-direction, respectively.
For an isotropic reinforced panel, the reinforcement degree will equal to each other, thus
Φ = Φx = Φy
(4.5)
where else an orthotropic reinforced panel is deﬁned as a panel with di↵erent reinforcement degrees
in each direction, thus
Φx 6= Φy
(4.6)
The inclination of the concrete compression, ✓, will depend on whether the panel is isotropic or
orthotropic reinforced. For an isotropic reinforced panel, the inclination will be constant, while the
angle for an orthotropic reinforced panel will vary. This is due to the fact, that the reinforcement in
an isotropic reinforced panel will yield simultaneously, while the orthotropic will yield in one direction
prior to the other. This will cause the angle to rotate towards the direction of the reinforcement which
will yield lastly. This means that the response curve of a panel will consist of maximum three phases,
given as; the elastic phase, the partial plastic phase and the ultimate plastic phase
The elastic phase is the phase where none of the reinforcement directions has yielded yet. When
one reinforcement direction starts to yield prior to the other direction, the partial plastic phase is
entered. As stated, this phase only exists for orthotropic disks, since orthotropic disks have di↵erent
reinforcement degrees in each direction. For both isotropic and orthotropic reinforced panels, both
reinforcement directions will start to yield at some point. This is referred to as the ultimate plastic
phase. In this phase, the stress will be constant under increasing strain.
In the following, the three phases will be investigated to ﬁnd the shear stress capacity for panels
subjected to pure shear and panels subjected to shear and biaxial compression/tension.
32
==PAGE:48==
4.1.1
Response curve for reinforced panels in pure shear
For a panel subjected to pure shear, as seen in Figure 4.1, the stresses applied to the element in the
(x, y)-direction will be equal to zero, thus
σx = σy = 0
(4.7)
τ
τ
τ
τ
y
x
Figure 4.1: Panel subjected to pure shear
This should be kept in mind when using the fundamental principles of mechanics of materials.
The model intends to ﬁnd the shear stress ⌧xy and the shear strain γxy for every load step. To do so,
the load steps will depend on an increasing compressive strain "c. From this, it will become possible
to model the theoretic stress and strength in the panel, which at the intersection point, will give the
theoretic shear stress capacity ⌧max and failure shear strain γmax for every panel.
In the study, when investigating orthotropic reinforced panels, the transverse reinforcement direction,
also called the y-direction, will contain a lower reinforcement degree than the longitudinal direction
(x-direction). This means, that the y-direction always yields prior to the x-direction. This knowledge
is used when establishing the equations used for the model. Never the less, equations for both cases
are walked through in the following sections.
Elastic phase
The elastic phase is given as the ﬁrst phase of the response curve where none of the reinforcement
directions are yielding.
In this phase, the angle ✓can be found from the equilibrium equations seen in Equation (2.30) and
(2.31). Given that the total stresses in the x- and y-direction are zero for pure shear, it leads to an
angle ✓of

Partial plastic phase
As stated in the previous section, the partial plastic phase applies, when one reinforcement direction
starts to yield prior to the other direction.
The inclination of the concrete compression will be found from the fact that the stresses in the steel are
governed by the rigid-plastic material behaviour. Therefore, the stresses cannot exceed the yielding
strength, fy. Thus, depending on which direction that yields ﬁrst, the inclination can be found from
the equilibrium equations as
For ﬁrst yielding being in the x-direction:

Ultimate plastic phase
When the last reinforcement direction starts to yield, the ultimate plastic phase is reached.
Here the ultimate shear stress, ⌧u becomes [7]
⌧u =

(4.17)
Equation (4.17) can also be derived from the equilibrium equations.
When the ultimate shear stress is reached, the inclination of the concrete compression, ✓, will be
constant, since both reinforcement directions are yielding. Furthermore, the concrete compressive
strain, "c, will become constant, since the ultimate shear force has been reached, while the transverse
concrete strain, ", will keep increasing, as given in the rigid-plastic theory.
The shear strain, γxy, is still found from Equation (2.20).

4.1.3
Panel shear strength
To use the above theories, where a rigid-plastic behaviour is assumed, the concrete strength, fc, will
have to be reduced by an e↵ectiveness factor, ⌫. The panel shear strength will be tested with the eight
di↵erent e↵ectiveness factors presented in Section 2.3.
The panel shear strength for the eight models can be found by using Equation (2.27) where the
concrete stress should be equal to ⌫fc
⌧c = ⌫fccos(✓)sin(✓)
(4.29)
The theoretic shear stress capacity of a given panel is found from the intersection between the response
curve and the panel shear strength. The intersection point is found by using a Matlab function made
by Douglas M. Schwarz [3], see Appendix D. The theoretic shear stress capacity is further investigated
in the next sections.
38
==PAGE:54==
4.2
Shear stress obtained from the model
All the equations from Section 4.1 has been implemented in a Matlab script to ﬁnd the theoretical
shear stress capacity for all the tested panels. The script can be found in Appendix D.
Two scripts have been made; one for pure shear and one for combined shear and biaxial stress. The
plots obtained from the two are similar to each other, and in the following, it will be presented how
the plots should be interpreted. Due to a large number of plots, only some of them will be presented,
while the rest can be seen in Appendix E.
4.2.1
Analysing the stress-strain plots of the model
In Figure 4.3 the response curve for PV22 along with the shear strength of the panel for the eight
tested ⌫-factors can be seen. PV22 is subjected to pure shear. Furthermore, the response curve from
the test is illustrated.
Figure 4.3: PV22
First of all, it is seen that the panel is an orthotropic reinforced panel. This is seen due to the three
phases that the response curve goes through. The elastic phase is seen as a linear relation between
the shear stress and the shear strain. When the ﬁrst reinforcement direction starts to yield, which
for the used tests are in the transverse direction (y-direction), the inclination of concrete compression
will start to vary. This is seen in the partial plastic phase, where the relation between the shear
stress and shear strain is slightly curving. When the last reinforcement direction starts to yield, i.e.
the longitudinal direction (x-direction), the shear stress will become constant under increasing shear
strain.
The panel will fail when the panel shear strength and the response curve of the panel intersect.
In Figure 4.3, eight panel shear strengths are tested due to the eight di↵erent e↵ectiveness factors
investigated. This means that eight di↵erent failure points will be obtained. These are the values
that will be compared to the failure value obtained in the experiments. Based on this comparison, the
e↵ectiveness factor with the best ﬁt will be chosen and used in further investigations.
39
==PAGE:55==
The response curve measured from the experiment is illustrated by the scatter points. For the panels
tested by Vecchio and Collins (as in Figure 4.3) the full response curve is given, while only the point
of failure is provided in the remaining experiments. By knowing the failure point from tests, a reliable
panel shear strength can be sought out. For the panels with a full response curve, it can be tested how
well the theory ﬁts with reality. In Figure 4.3 the ﬁt seems rather good. The scatter seems to have a
rather steep increase in shear stress, until about 2 MPa where a kink is seen. From here the scatter
approaches the theoretical response curve and failure happens fairly close to the theoretic failure. This
is further discussed in the discussion.
Depending on the which ⌫-factor that was used, some will indicate that failure will happen after
yielding in one direction, as seen in Figure 4.3. However, no yielding was observed in the experiment,
as seen in Table 3.1.
(a) PV20
(b) PV27
Figure 4.4: Stress-strain plots
PV20 was subjected to pure shear. In Figure 4.4a it is seen that failure will happen after yielding in
one direction, which complies well with test observations (see Table 3.1). Furthermore, it is seen that
the reinforcement degree in the transverse compared to the longitudinal direction must be greater
for PV20 than PV22, which can be observed by the larger partial plastic phase seen in Figure 4.4a
compared to Figure 4.3. This is conﬁrmed when looking at the reinforcement ratios in Table 3.7.
PV27 is subjected to pure shear. In Figure 4.4b it is seen, that the response curve for panel PV27
only consists of two phases; the elastic phase and ultimate plastic phase. This means that the panel
was isotropic reinforced. Besides that, it is seen that failure happened before the steel yielded, i.e. the
elastic phase. This was also observed in the experiment, as seen in Table 3.1.
Figure 4.5 is showing the response curve for panel PV25. PV25 is isotropic reinforced and subjected
to combined shear and compression.
40
==PAGE:56==
Figure 4.5: PV25
It is seen from Figure 4.5, that the failure will be governed by concrete crushing. By comparing the
panel shear strength curves for PV25 with PV20, PV22 and PV27, it is observed, that the failure in
PV25 is found on the steeper part of the shear strength curve. Failure in this area is only observed
for seven cases (PV9, S-21, S-31, S-32 and PV23-PV25). Here, the shear stress found by ⌫1 varies to
a greater extent from the shear stress obtained by the remaining e↵ectiveness factors.
4.2.2
Model shear stress capacity
In Table 4.1 and Table 4.2 the theoretical shear stress capacity is seen. These values will be compared
to the values from test. Though, only the ones with failure prior to both directions has yielded are
sought out. If the predicted strength is governed by yielding of reinforcement in both directions, it
will not be critical to concrete crushing.
In Table 4.1 it is seen, that for panels subjected to pure shear, the e↵ectiveness factors ⌫2 and ⌫6 has
the highest shear stress values, while ⌫1 and ⌫5 has the lowest. This is comparable to what is observed
in Table 4.2 for panels subjected to combined shear and biaxial compression/tension. Though, all
shear stress capacities are fairly close. It is seen, that when comparing ⌫1 and ⌫2 the shear stress is
lower for ⌫1. Furthermore, when comparing ⌫3, ⌫4 and ⌫5 it is seen that the lowest shear stress is
found for ⌫5 and the highest shear stress is found for ⌫3. Likewise, ⌫8 has the lowest shear stress while
⌫6 has the highest, when comparing ⌫6, ⌫7 and ⌫8. Finally, it is seen that when comparing ⌫3 with ⌫6,
⌫4 with ⌫7 and ⌫5 with ⌫8, ⌫6, ⌫7 and ⌫8 will result in the highest shear stress.

4.3
Comparison of test and model
By comparing the theoretical values from Table 4.1 and Table 4.2 with the values from Table 3.6 and
Table 3.7, an analysis can be made of which e↵ectiveness factor that gives the best ﬁt. The analysis
will be divided into panels subjected to pure shear and panels subjected to combined shear and biaxial
compression/tension.
4.3.1
Panels subjected to pure shear
In Table 4.3 a comparison between the model and the experiments can be seen as a calculated mean
and standard deviation for ⌧max(test)/⌧max(theory). The calculation is seen in Appendix F. To have
the best ﬁt the mean should be close to 1, while the standard deviation should be as low as possible.

From the table, it is seen that all e↵ectiveness factors gives a rather good ﬁt. But it is also seen that
the theoretical shear stress capacities are slightly on the unsafe side since the mean value is below 1
for all eight cases. Though, since the mean values are so close to 1, it is estimated that the ﬁt is safe
enough.
When looking further into the numbers, it is seen that ⌫1 gives the best ﬁt, since the mean is the
closest to 1, while the standard deviation has the lowest value among the eight factors. Though, since
the e↵ectiveness factor ⌫1 has an interaction factor with k1 = 1.2, it is questionable how well this
e↵ectiveness factor will ﬁt for panels subjected to e.g. biaxial compression. By comparing ⌫3, ⌫4 and
⌫5 it is seen that ⌫5 has the best ﬁt, but also the greatest standard deviation. This is also the case
when comparing ⌫6, ⌫7 and ⌫8, where ⌫8 has the best ﬁt, but also the greatest standard deviation.
Lastly, it is seen, that by comparing ⌫4 with ⌫7, the best ﬁt is found for ⌫4.
To further investigate how the shear stress capacity is a↵ected, it has been plotted for various pa-
rameters. This can be seen in Figure 4.7 for the theoretical values obtained by ⌫1. In Figure 4.6 the
legend can be seen for the various colours and symbols used in the following plots. The plots have
been made for all ⌫-factors and can be found in Appendix G.

==PAGE:64==
The general trend of the predicted shear capacity shown in Table 4.6 and 4.7 is that all predicted
shear capacities from the models correlate well with the shear capacity observed from the experiments.
Furthermore, it is observed from the two tables, that the models predict wrongly from the specimens
tested by Vecchio and Chan. This also applies to specimen PA1 and PA2 tested by Collins, Vecchio and
Aspiotis, but here it is stated in the article [14], that localised yielding of the longitudinal reinforcement
may have accompanied the concrete failure, which implies that the failure mode could be YY+YX+CC
as well as YY+CC.
For pure shear, it is observed in Table 4.7, that the model applying ⌫1 predicts slightly better than
the other seven. Furthermore, it is observed that the model using ⌫6 gives the worst prediction.
For panels in combined shear and biaxial compression/tension, all eight combinations had equally
good failure mode predictions.

==PAGE:66==
Chapter 5
Method of calculation for shear
capacity of panels
In Section 4.1, the eight equations for the e↵ectiveness factor were evaluated by establishing the
complete response curve and the shear strength in the concrete panel. Now, a di↵erent approach is
used to assess the e↵ectiveness factor. Here, the e↵ectiveness factor for various panels are found by
iteration, and afterwards, the predicted shear capacities are compared ﬁrstly with test results and
lastly with the model results.
5.1
Calculation procedure
In this case, only panels subjected to pure shear are considered. The reinforcement design is found
iterative because the e↵ectiveness factor depends on the transverse strain and the inclination of con-
crete compression. Again, it has to be noted, that the predicted shear strength for the panels is only
interesting when the panels are failing by concrete crushing (elastic phase) or concrete crushing and
yielding of reinforcement in one direction (partial plastic phase). It is not interesting to investigate
panels, where the shear strength is governed by yielding in both directions (ultimate plastic phase)
since concrete crushing is not critical.
It is assumed in the calculation, that the reinforcement in the y-direction will yield before yielding in
the x-direction
⇢xfyx ≥⇢yfyy
(5.1)

It is seen from Table 5.1, that the shear strength for the panels obtained using the eight combinations

strength obtained with ⌫4 and ⌫7, it is seen that the shear strength found by using the data from

⌫7 is applied.

Chapter 6
Ductility
In the following chapter, the ductility of reinforced panels is investigated. The calculations are solely
based on the model using the ⌫6-factor. The remaining e↵ectiveness factors will not be investigated.
Ductility is given as the period of which plastic deformations occur in a given material [7]. Ductile
materials can thus be described as materials which give a warning before an actual break. This can,
for instance, be seen under a tensile test using steel dog-bones. Here the specimen will locally narrow
its cross-section, also called necking, at the point where it will later fracture. The phenomenon is
illustrated in Figure 6.1.
Figure 6.1: Tensile test of a steel dog-bone. Necking happens prior to fracture [4]
When comparing the stress-strain curve of steel and the rigid-plastic curve, as illustrated in Fig-
ure 2.10a, it is seen that the behaviour is quite similar. Thus, the rigid-plastic behaviour, and thereby
the plastic theories, is a good approximation for steel. This is not the case for concrete. As mentioned
earlier, concrete becomes more and more brittle the greater the strength becomes. Where else a duc-
tile material gives a warning before failure, a brittle material does not. To compensate for this, the
e↵ectiveness factor should always be used when using plastic theories.
In the following sections, an introduction will be made of how the ductility is measured. Then, a
parametric study is performed, to investigate which e↵ect di↵erent steel and concrete parameters have
on the ductility. Lastly, it is investigated how well the model complies with the experimental data.
6.1
Ductility index
Given that ductility is deﬁned as the period in a given material where plastic deformations occur, it
is only of interest to investigate panels where failure happens in the partial- or ultimate plastic phase.
57
==PAGE:73==
The ductility will in this study be measured by an ductility index, which is calculated as the relation
between the shear strain at failure, γmax, and the shear strain at ﬁrst yielding of the reinforcement,
γy.
Ductility index = γmax
γy
(6.1)
Thus, if a failure occurs in the elastic phase, the ductility index will become less than 1, which means
that the ductility index can be regarded as equal to zero ductility.
The maximum shear strain γmax is found at the intersection between the calculated response curve
and the panel shear strength, see Figure 6.2. It is tested if there is any relation between the ductility
index and di↵erent material parameters.
Point of first yielding, y
Maximum shear strain for class B and C, max
Maximum shear strain for class A, max
Figure 6.2: Estimating ductility of a reinforced concrete panel
In some cases, the steel might fail before the concrete. To take this into account, the shear strain for
steel class A, B and C are calculated. The characteristic ultimate strain of the three steel classes are
given in DS/EN 1992-1-1 as [2]
"uk,A ≥0.25
"uk,B ≥0.50
"uk,C ≥0.75
From this, it is tested whether the ductility index is governed by the steel strength or the concrete
strength. In Figure 6.2 it is seen that the failure for class A steel would be governed by the steel, while
it for class B- and C steel would be governed by the concrete strength. To convert the characteristic
strains into shear strains Equation (2.17) is used where "y = "uk. When isolating for γmax it gives
58
==PAGE:74==
γmax = 2("uk + "c)tan(✓)
(6.2)
Here "c is the principal compressive strain when both rebars are yielding, and ✓is the inclination of
concrete compression after both rebars have yielded.
6.2
Parametric study
A parametric study was performed, using the ⌫6-factor, to investigate the inﬂuence each parameter
had on the ductility index. In the study, the x-direction always had the largest amount of reinforce-
ment ratio, i.e. ⇢x ≥⇢y, while the reinforcement ratios in the y-direction was chosen so that the
reinforcement degree ratios remained constant.

In Table 6.1 the ductility index from the parametric study is shown. Four di↵erent concrete strengths
(fc [MPa]) and steel strengths (fy [MPa]) are tested along with three reinforcement degree ratios
(Φx/Φy [-]) and reinforcement ratio in the x-direction (⇢x [-]). The reinforcement degree ratio, Φx/Φy,
is tested for
Φx/Φy = 1 ⇡cot(✓) = 1
(6.3)
Φx/Φy = 4 ⇡cot(✓) = 2
(6.4)
60
==PAGE:76==
Φx/Φy = 9 ⇡cot(✓) = 3
(6.5)
while the concrete and steel strength has been chosen as typical strength parameters used in Denmark.
Isotropic reinforced panels
For isotropic reinforced panels, the reinforcement degree ratio will be equal to 1. Here an assessment
of the other parameters inﬂuence on the ductility index, DI, will be conducted. It is seen from Table
6.1, that DI increases with increasing concrete strength, fc.
Furthermore, it is observed that an increase in the reinforcement ratio in the x-direction, ⇢x, results
in a lower value for DI. Likewise, it is seen, that DI decreases, when the steel strength, fy increases.
For the three steel classes, class A, B and C, it applies for most of the cases, that DI is identical
when all other parameters are constant. The only exceptions are when ⇢x = 0.01, fc ≥40 MPa and
fy 275 MPa, where DI is identical for class B and C and lower DI-value are obtained for class A.
Furthermore, class A steel have lower values for the cases, where ⇢x = 0.01, fc = 50 MPa and fy 355
MPa.
Orthotropic reinforced panels
For orthotropic reinforced panels, the reinforcement degree varies from 1. Again, it is observed for
most of the cases, that DI increases for increasing fc. However, it is observed for the cases, where
Φx/Φy=4, ⇢x = 0.01 gives a lower DI, when fc strength is higher than 40 MPa. Likewise, it is observed,
that DI gets lower for the cases, where Φx/Φy=9, ⇢x = 0.01, fc >40 MPa and fy 275 MPa.
Additionally, it is observed that DI decreases with increasing fy. This also applies for ⇢x.
For the three steel classes, it is observed that steel class A, B and C have identical values, when
Φx/Φy=4, ⇢x = 0.02, fc = 20 MPa and when Φx/Φy=4, ⇢x = 0.03, fc = 30 MPa. For ⇢x = 0.01, it is
seen, that DI is only identical for steel class B and C, when fc = 20 MPa or fc = 30 MPa and fy ≥
355 MPa. Also, it is observed that steel class B and C have a greater DI compared with steel class A.
When Φx/Φy=9, it is observed that DI is identical for the cases, where ⇢x = 0.02, fc = 20 MPa and
fy ≥275 MPa for steel class A, B and C. Likewise, is DI identical when ⇢x = 0.02 or 0.03 for steel
class B and C except when ⇢x=0.02, fc ≥40 MPa and fy 275 MPa. For ⇢x = 0.01, it is seen that
steel class B and C have identical DI, when fc = 20 MPa and fy ≥275 MPa. Again, it is observed
that steel class C have the greatest DI and steel class A have the lowest.
Comparing isotropic- with orthotropic reinforced panels
The general trend, for both isotropic and orthotropic reinforced panels, is that increasing fc results in
increasing DI. Furthermore, it is observed that increasing ⇢x and fy result in decreasing DI. Addition-
ally, steel class C will either have higher or identical DI-value with steel class A or B. It is observed,
that orthotropic panels have a higher DI compared with isotropic panels.
For Φx/Φy, the trend is a little bit more unclear. It is seen that in most cases, the isotropic case, i.e.
Φx/Φy = 1, will have the lowest DI. For high reinforcement ratios, ⇢x, the ductility index seems to
increase with increasing Φx/Φy, though, this is not even always the case. In Figure 6.3 an overview
has been made of how the reinforcement degree ratio, compared between equal reinforcement ratios,
is a↵ected.
61
==PAGE:77==
Figure 6.3: Inﬂuence of Φx/Φy for a constant ⇢x.
Blue colour has the lowest
ductility index of the three compared values, green colour has the 2nd lowest
ductility index while red colour has the highest ductility index
When looking more into the ductility indexes, it is seen that the highest values are obtained for the
orthotropic case Φx/Φy=4 (⇡cot(✓)=2) with reinforcement ratio ⇢x = 0.01 for high concrete strengths
and class C steel. On the other hand, it is seen that the lowest ductility index is obtained when the
panel is isotropic reinforced, i.e. Φx/Φy = 1, the reinforcement ratio and steel strength is high, and
the concrete strength is low. The highest and lowest ductility index case from the parametric study is
illustrated in Figure 6.4. Figure 6.4b are showing, that the panel is failing in the elastic phase. When
this happens, the ductility index will be less than 1 given the index deﬁnition, which means that it
will be governed by concrete crushing and ductility will thereby become irrelevant.
62
==PAGE:78==
(a) Highest ductility index from parametric study:
Φx/Φy = 4, ⇢x = 0.01, fc = 40, fy = 235MPa and
class C steel
(b) Lowest ductility index from parametric study:
Φx/Φy = 1, ⇢x = 0.03, fc = 20 and fy = 420 MPa
Figure 6.4: Highest and lowest ductility index obtained in the parametric study
6.3
Shear strain at failure
In the following the shear strain at failure, γmax, found in the model is compared to the one found in
the experiments.
The ultimate characteristic strength of the steel could only be collected from the experiments by
Vecchio and Collins. Therefore, only the failure shear strain in the experiments of Vecchio and Collins
has been divided with the shear strain predicted in the model. The calculated mean and standard
deviation is presented in Table 6.2, and the calculation is presented in more details in Table M.17 in
Appendix M.

64
==PAGE:80==
Chapter 7
Discussion
In Appendix G, it is seen that the stress-strain plots show a fairly good ﬁt with the experimental data.
Some deviate more than others, but this is to be expected since human errors and small inaccuracies
in the test set-up might cause some variations. Besides that, the assumptions made to simplify the
model might also cause deviations. One of the assumptions that can cause deviation might be the
assumption of inﬁnite concrete strength, which means that Hooke’s law applies at all times, where in
reality, it is seen from the stress-strain plot of concrete given in Figure 2.11, that the E-modulus will
change with the strain. If this could be implemented in the model, the inclination of the response
curve would change, and perhaps become more precise.
Furthermore, it is seen, that the experimental response curve seen in the Vecchio & Collins panels,
seems to follow the theoretic response curve rather nicely. Though, as mentioned earlier, they seem
to have a more steep inclination in shear stress at the beginning than the theoretic response curve,
until a kink appears, which causes the two curves to close in again. The di↵erent inclination of the
two response curves are due to the theoretic curve being calculated as the concrete being uncracked,
while in reality, cracks appear, which is seen as a kink in the response curve.
From Chapter 4 and 5, it is seen from Table 5.3, that the predicted shear strengths for pure shear
panels from the model and the method of calculation are fairly identical. Likewise, it is observed, that
the results from the sections about the comparison between model and experiment and the compar-
ison between method of calculation and experiment have identical conclusions when comparing the
ratios for various e↵ectiveness factors. This could imply, that the predicted shear strength obtained
from the model and the method of calculation would be the same. This also makes sense, since the
method of calculation and the model both are based on the rigid-plastic theory. For practical use, the
method of calculation would be a good alternative to the model, since the model implementation is
fairly advanced and time-consuming to establish.
In Chapter 4 and 5, it is observed that the highest value of the shear strength in each panel is ob-
tained when either applying ⌫2, ⌫3 or ⌫6 and the lowest shear strength are found when applying either
⌫1, ⌫5 or ⌫8. As stated in Section 2.3 the e↵ectiveness factor depends on the brittleness factor and
the interaction factor between steel and concrete. Therefore, it makes sense, that the highest shear
strength is observed, when either ⌫2, ⌫3 or ⌫6 are applied, since they are obtained with a relatively
smaller k2-value. In the same way, it makes sense, when ⌫1, ⌫5 and ⌫8 are applied, that the lowest
shear strength is observed, since ⌫5 and ⌫8 are obtained with the highest k2-value and ⌫1 is obtained
with the largest k1-value among the eight combinations. When comparing the shear strength obtained
by ⌫3 and ⌫6, it is seen that the shear strength are identical for the specimen examined by Collins
65
==PAGE:81==
and Vecchio, where all the specimen have a lower compression strength than 30 MPa. Otherwise, it
is seen that the shear strength for ⌫6 is greater than ⌫3, which is due to the brittleness factor and in
particular, fc0.
Furthermore, it is observed that the best ﬁt for pure shear between experiments and the two theories
are obtained, when ⌫1 is used. However, it is also observed that the shear strength obtained with
⌫1 for panels subjected to combined shear and biaxial compression/tension has the worst ﬁt, where
a better ﬁt is observed for the shear strength obtained with ⌫2. This could imply, that k1-value =
1.2 has a bad ﬁt for anything other than pure shear panels. In most cases, it is observed, from the
stress-strain plots obtained from the model, that the shear strength for pure shear panels is found
after the steeper part of the shear strength curve. To asses the k1-value more thorough, it is needed to
collect data from more experiments, where the shear strength of the panel is obtained in the steeper
part of the shear strength curve. In this thesis, only seven panels have been collected.
In Section 4.3 the model’s predicted shear strength were compared to the experimental failure value.
Here an analysis was performed separately for panels subjected to pure shear and panels subjected
to biaxial compression/tension. Furthermore, an analysis was performed for the two subjection types
combined. From the three analysis, it was seen, that the best ﬁt varied a lot with the subjection type.
For pure shear ⌫1 seemed to have the best ﬁt, while ⌫2 and ⌫6 had the worst. For combined shear and
biaxial compression/tension, this was exactly the opposite.
When calculating the mean and standard deviation for all the tested panels regardless of the load
type, ⌫5 seemed to have the best ﬁt. Though, due to a large number of pure shear panels compared to
panels subjected to combined shear and biaxial compression/tension, the mean values and standard
deviation might be misleading, since the calculation was not based on a weighted mean and standard
deviation for the two load types.
In the tables, it is seen, that the panels subjected to pure shear, in general, has a smaller deviation
than for panels subjected to combined shear and biaxial compression/tension. Again, it is important
to note, that the amount of data collected for panels in pure shear is greater than for the panels
subjected to combined shear and biaxial compression/tension. Therefore, it would be of interest if
more data could be collected for panels subjected to combined shear and biaxial compression/tension.
From Chapter 6, it is observed, that a higher concrete strength results in a higher ductility index. This
makes sense since a higher concrete strength will give a higher principal tensile strain and therefore
larger shear stress, due to the lower reduction factor of the interaction between steel and concrete, and
a greater shear strain. However, it has to be noted, that after 40 MPa, the increase of the ductility
index would be less, because of the brittleness factor. In the same way, it makes sense that greater
steel strength and reinforcement ratio will results in lower ductility index, because of the lower prin-
cipal tensile strain. Therefore, for the case where the reinforcement ratio in the x-direction is small,
concrete strength is relatively high and steel strength is relatively low, the failure for class A steel will
be governed by steel failure.
It was expected that orthotropic reinforced panels would have a larger ductility index because the
index is calculated as the ratio between ﬁrst yielding point and failure, and that isotropic panels only
have one yielding point. Furthermore, a low reinforcement ratio ⇢x for orthotropic reinforced panels
will mean that the reinforcement ratio in the y-direction is even smaller, causing the reinforcement
to yield at a small stress. When combining this with a class C steel and a high strength concrete, it
will either be the high strength concrete or the highly ductile steel that dominates the failure point,
resulting in a large ductility index.
It is seen that the lowest ductility index is obtained when the panel is isotropic reinforced, i.e.
Φx/Φy = 1, the reinforcement ratio and steel strength is high, and the concrete strength is low.
66
==PAGE:82==
In these cases, the steel classes does not matter, since all panels will fail in the elastic phase. When
this happens, the ductility index will be less than 1 given the index deﬁnition, which means that it
will be governed by concrete crushing and ductility will thereby become irrelevant.
When investigating how well the shear strain predictions ﬁt, it is seen that the results are on the
unsafe side, since the mean value is found to be 0.7, which indicates that the predicted maximum
shear strain in most cases are larger than what is observed in the test. Besides that, a rather large
deviation is observed. In particular, it is seen from Figure 6.5, that isotropic reinforced panels has
a large deviation. However, when investigating the three isotropic reinforced panels, it is seen from
Figure 6.6a that the test of PV4 might have stopped before an actual failure since the drop in shear
stress is almost undetectable. This could also be implied in the stress-strain curve of PV16. From
Figure 6.6b a rather large gap can be seen between the last observation points for PV6. The actual
failure point might, therefore, be somewhere in between the last two observations.
Based on this, it is diﬃcult to evaluate whether the model prediction for shear strain is reliable.
Therefore, it is suggested that the parametric study performed for the ductility index only is used as
a parametric analysis and not as a reference work.
67
==PAGE:83==
68
==PAGE:84==
Chapter 8
Conclusion
During the project, it was found that the stress ﬁeld assumed in the rigid-plastic theory in combination
with the constitutive equations, compatibility requirements and equilibrium equation indicates a good
estimation of the shear strength capacity in orthogonally reinforced panels. It was furthermore found,
that the eight suggested e↵ectiveness factors all had a rather nice ﬁt, but that the best ﬁt for panels
subjected to pure shear was ⌫1 given as
⌫1 =
3
r30
fc
·
1
1.2 + 80"1
Though, it was observed that it was slightly on the unsafe side. ⌫6 had the best ﬁt for panels subjected
to combined shear and biaxial compression/tension, which was given as
⌫6 =
3
r40
fc
·
1
1 + 90"1
Here it was observed that it was on the safe side. It was investigated whether one of the eight suggested
e↵ectiveness factors could apply to both load types. Here it was found, that the ﬁt for the two cases
were more or less opposite to each other, though, with that in mind the best ﬁt was found to be
⌫5 =
3
r30
fc
·
1
1 + 110"1
with a mean value of 0.997 and a standard deviation of 0.136 when comparing the predicted shear
capacity from the model with the shear capacity measured from experiments.
When testing the iterative method of calculation for estimation of the shear strength capacity, it
was found that the prediction was almost identical to the one predicted from the theoretical model.
Therefore, it is concluded, that the method of calculation is rather good if only the shear strength
capacity is sought out since this method is better suited for practical use.
Unfortunately, the obtained maximum shear strains from the theoretical model did not seem to ﬁt
very well with the experimental maximum shear strains. It could both be concluded that the variation
was rather large and that the predicted shear strain values in most cases were on the unsafe side to
69
==PAGE:85==
use. The parametric study, which was performed to evaluate the e↵ect of each parameter in relation
to the ductility index, is thus only suitable as a parametric analysis and not as a reference of work.
8.1
Future works
In the thesis, the concrete is assumed to have inﬁnite concrete strength, which means that no cracks
will occur before failure. In reality, concrete do not have a linear-elastic material behaviour until
failure. Therefore, in the future, the theoretical model could be extended with a modiﬁed concrete
elasticity modulus which is related to the principal tensile and compressive strain. Furthermore, it
could be relevant to compare the predicted shear strength obtained from the theoretical model with
shear strength found in rigid-plastic FEM-analysis.
Since the analysis for panels subjected to combined shear and biaxial compression/tension is based
on rather few test panels, it is suggested that more tests should be conducted in the future, to make
the data more representative. If so, it would also be possible to investigate if panels subjected to
combined shear and biaxial compression had a di↵erent ﬁt than that of panels subjected to combined
shear and biaxial tension.
From Figure 4.5, it is observed that the shear strength obtained from ⌫1 varies more than the shear
strength obtained using the other e↵ectiveness factors. This means, that the shear strength for PV25
is obtained where the theoretical response curve intersects with the steeper part of the shear strength
curve. In this area, the deviation between the ⌫-factors are caused by the di↵erent k1-values. If more
experiments could be conducted, where failure happened in this area, it would be possible to asses
the k1-value more thoroughly. Therefore, it is interesting to perform a parametric study, on how to
obtain a failure in this area.

Appendix B
Determining the e↵ectiveness factor
During the project period, multiple combinations of the e↵ectiveness factor were tested. All com-
binations were based on the formulas seen in Equation (2.41) and Equation (2.42).
Three of the
combinations were tested on the request of Linh Hoang, to test whether the factors, that until now
had been considered in the recently proposed equation, had the best possible ﬁt.
The parameters which can be varied in the two equations are
- The boundary between ductile and brittle concrete, fc0
- The two k-values deﬁning the interaction loss between steel and concrete in relation to the transverse
strain; k1 and k2
At ﬁrst, the following e↵ectiveness factor was suggested
⌫= ⌘fc⌘" =

unwanted.

of the remaining parameters (fc0 and k2) three values of each parameter was chosen. These are seen

Where YY = yielding in the y-direction, YX = yielding in the x-direction and CC = concrete crushing
the best ﬁt, while it for panels subjected to combined shear and biaxial compression/tension seems
to be fc0 = 40 MPa. However, it is observed that fc0 = 20 MPa has the worst ﬁt, when k2 = 100 for
combined shear and biaxial compression tension and for k2 = 150 for both load cases, therefore is 20
MPa discarded, which leaves 30 MPa and 40 MPa as the most reasonable combinations. Therefore,
these will be checked, along with a couple of new suggestions, to see if it is possible to predict a more
precise e↵ectiveness factor. The chosen values are presented in section 2.3.3.

Appendix C
Finding ✓numerically
For orthotropic panels in shear and biaxial compression/tension, ✓was found numerically in the zone
where one reinforcement had yielded while the other had not. The ✓at ﬁrst yielding (yielding in the
y-direction) and at last yielding (yielding in the x-direction) was found in Maple and then inserted in
Excel to ﬁnd related ✓and "c values.

%% Disks in pure shear
% Input parametre
filename = 'Master_Excel_ark.xlsx';
[num,txt,raw] = xlsread(filename,'Pure_Shear_Data','B62:O62');

t_test = num(12); % MPa

% t_test = [0.997,2.028,2.75,3.483,4.186,4.9,5.361,5.817,6.24];
 
%% REINFORCEMENT
% Reinforcement degree
Phiy = rhoy*fyy/fc; %(Slideshow)
Phix = rhox*fyx/fc; %(Slideshow)
% Psi-values
Psiy = rhoy*Esy/Ec; %(Beton 2 - 6.13.2.7)
Psix = rhox*Esx/Ec; %(Beton 2 - 6.13.2.7)
% Angle of the concrete pressure
Theta = atan(((1+Psix)/(1+Psiy)*Psiy/Psix)^(1/4)); %Radians (Beton 2 - 6.13.2.8)
 
%% Pure Shear
ec = [0:(0.0001-0)/100:0.01];
 
% Yielding of the reinforcement
ec_fx = rhox*fyx/(Ec*cos(Theta)^2); %for pure shear (Beton 2 - 6.13.2.4)
ec_fy = rhoy*fyy/(Ec*sin(Theta)^2); %for pure shear (Beton 2 - 6.13.2.4)
 
% The minimum value of min(ec_fx,ec_fy) will be the reinforcement with the 
% given direction that yields first. If they are equal to each other, the 
% panel is isotropic and the reinforcement in both directions will yield 
% simultaneously. 
 
ec_f = min(ec_fx,ec_fy);
 
tu = sqrt(Phiy*Phix)*fc; % Maximum shear stress in the reinforcement
 
t2 = zeros(1,length(ec));
 
% ts is the vector for the response curve corresponding to the stresses in
% the steel, assuming infinite concrete strength. Depending on if the
% reinforcement are isotropic or orthotropic, the vector will have 2-3 stages
% Stage 1: Elastic zone, steel has not yielded
% Stage 2: First direction of steel will yield for orthotropic panels
% Stage 3: Both steel has yielded

for i = 1:length(ec)
    if ec(i)<ec_f
        Theta_f(i) = Theta; % Radians
        t1(i) = Ec*ec(i)*cos(Theta_f(i))*sin(Theta_f(i)); % (Beton 2 - 6.13.2.4)
        ts(i) = t1(i);
        esx(i)= (Ec*ec(i)*cos(Theta_f(i))^2/rhox)/Esx; % (Beton 2 - 6.12.2.22)
        esy(i)= (Ec*ec(i)*sin(Theta_f(i))^2/rhoy)/Esy; % (Beton 2 - 6.12.2.22)
        e(i) = esx(i)+esy(i)+ec(i); % (Article p. 221)
        y(i) = 2*(ec(i)+e(i))*cos(Theta_f(i))*sin(Theta_f(i)); % (Beton 2 - 6.13.2.2)
    else
        if Phix<Phiy
              Theta_f(i) = acos(sqrt((fyx*rhox)/(Ec*ec(i)))); % (Beton 2 - 6.13.2.4)
              esy(i)= (Ec*ec(i)*sin(Theta_f(i))^2/rhoy)/Esy; % (Beton 2 - 6.12.2.22)
              e(i) = tan(Theta_f(i))^2*(esy(i)+ec(i))+esy(i); % (Artikel s. 221)
        elseif Phix>Phiy 
              Theta_f(i) = asin(sqrt((fyy*rhoy)/(Ec*ec(i)))); % (Beton 2 - 6.13.2.4)
              esx(i)= (Ec*ec(i)*cos(Theta_f(i))^2/rhox)/Esx; % (Beton 2 - 6.12.2.22)
              e(i) = (esx(i)+ec(i))/tan(Theta_f(i))^2+esx(i); % (Artikel s. 221)
        else 
              Theta_f(i) = Theta; % Isotropic
              esx(i)= (Ec*ec(i)*cos(Theta_f(i))^2/rhox)/Esx; % (Beton 2 - 6.12.2.22)
              esy(i)= (Ec*ec(i)*sin(Theta_f(i))^2/rhoy)/Esy; % (Beton 2 - 6.12.2.22)
              e(i) = esx(i)+esy(i)+ec(i); % (Article p. 221)
        end
        t2(i) = Ec*ec(i)*cos(Theta_f(i))*sin(Theta_f(i)); % (Beton 2 - 6.13.2.4)
        ts(i) = t2(i);
        y(i) = 2*(ec(i)+e(i))*cos(Theta_f(i))*sin(Theta_f(i)); % (Beton 2 - 6.13.2.2)
    end
    if t2(i)>tu
        ts(i) = tu; 
        % Theta after second yielding is found  
        idx(i) = find(ts(i)==tu,i,'first'); % Vector of 0 og 1 values
        idx_t = find(idx==1,i,'first'); % Vector finding the position of the 1-values 
        Theta_f(i) = Theta_f(idx_t(1)); % Finding the constant theta value after both 
directions has yielded
        ec(i) = (tu/(cos(Theta_f(i))*sin(Theta_f(i))))/Ec; % (6.13.2.23)
        e(i) = e(i-1)+0.001;
        y(i) = 2*(ec(i)+e(i))*cos(Theta_f(i))*sin(Theta_f(i)); % (Beton 2 - 6.13.2.2)
    else ts(i) = ts(i);
    end
    
    % Panel shear strength, nu_1
    eta_e1(i) = 1/(1.2+80*e(i)); % (EC) 
    eta_fc1(i) = min(1,(30/fc)^(1/3)); % Evaluating brittleness of concrete
    ny1(i) = eta_e1(i)*eta_fc1(i); % (EC)
    tc1(i) = ny1(i)*fc*cos(Theta_f(i))*sin(Theta_f(i));
    
    % Panel shear strength, nu_2
    eta_e2(i) = 1/(1+80*e(i)); % (EC)
    eta_fc2(i) = min(1,(30/fc)^(1/3)); % Evaluating brittleness of concrete
    ny2(i) = eta_e2(i)*eta_fc2(i); % (EC)
    tc2(i) = ny2(i)*fc*cos(Theta_f(i))*sin(Theta_f(i));
    
    % Panel shear strength, nu_3
    eta_e3(i) = 1/(1+90*e(i)); % (EC)
